<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12753548674728697233">The children in this book defy the stereotypes of urban youth too frequently presented by the media. Tender, generous and often religiously devout, they speak with eloquence and honesty about the poverty and racial isolation that have wounded but not hardened them. The book does not romanticize or soften the effects of violence and sickness. One fourth of the child-bearing</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12753548674728697233">The children in this book defy the stereotypes of urban youth too frequently presented by the media. Tender, generous and often religiously devout, they speak with eloquence and honesty about the poverty and racial isolation that have wounded but not hardened them. The book does not romanticize or soften the effects of violence and sickness. One fourth of the child-bearing women in the neighborhoods where these children live test positive for HIV. Pediatric AIDs, life-consuming fires and gang rivalries take a high toll. Several children die during the year in which this narrative takes place.<br /><br />A gently written work, "Amazing Grace" asks questions that are at once political and theological. What is the value of a child's life? What exactly do we plan to do with those whom we appear to have defined as economically and humanly superfluous? How cold -- how cruel, how tough -- do we dare be?</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12753548674728697233">...more</a>

      </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11902526" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/395947-lindsay" class="left imgcol" title="Lindsay"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1201016111p2/395947.jpg" alt="Lindsay" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/11902526?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 10, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/395947-lindsay" class="user" title="Lindsay">Lindsay</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">no one</span></div>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lindsay by: </span>
      required reading

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer11902526">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7089154954117003748">While the content of the book is very heart wrenching, and the statistics and the accounts Kozol provides are all very real, I hated this book. What troubles me is not the book, but its author Jonathan Kozol. Kozol motives are sincere and genuine but it always takes a sympathetic white man to expose the world. Which is almost unnerving as it is sad. On a whole, most of the white community in New York City doesn’t ever see what is happening around them; a train ride away. It’s as if the horrors o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7089154954117003748">While the content of the book is very heart wrenching, and the statistics and the accounts Kozol provides are all very real, I hated this book. What troubles me is not the book, but its author Jonathan Kozol. Kozol motives are sincere and genuine but it always takes a sympathetic white man to expose the world. Which is almost unnerving as it is sad. On a whole, most of the white community in New York City doesn’t ever see what is happening around them; a train ride away. It’s as if the horrors of the world are blurred into the background. That is until; one of their own spends a night in a homeless shelter, and writes of the rapes and murders that occur. Only until then, does the help, reform, sympathy and money come in. However, if a white man is what it takes, than a white man it shall be. If allowing an outsider to record and rewrite your life is the necessary meanings to improving your situation, then by all means whore yourself out to the white man. I fully understand that Kozol is only trying to help those less fortunate, but when will it be the blacks turn to help their own? </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7089154954117003748">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_119287590">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/119287590" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1824844-nancy" class="left imgcol" title="Nancy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1327324853p2/1824844.jpg" alt="Nancy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/119287590?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 01, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1824844-nancy" class="user" title="Nancy">Nancy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1824844-nancy?shelf=breakkk-11" class="actionLinkLite">breakkk-11</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1824844-nancy?shelf=12th-grade" class="actionLinkLite">12th-grade</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer119287590">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10769589764513699544">The crucial realities of a neighborhood’s life districted in South Bronx, New York City were told by Jonathan Kozol. Through his novel, Amazing Grace, he provided the true side and enables the wealthy privileged classes to witness the harsh conditions the poor had gone through. Because of this inequality issue which has been getting worse, readers are able to put their shoes in the lives of the poor. Such injustice drove to the huge gap between the rich and poor. More importantly, all these unfa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10769589764513699544">The crucial realities of a neighborhood’s life districted in South Bronx, New York City were told by Jonathan Kozol. Through his novel, Amazing Grace, he provided the true side and enables the wealthy privileged classes to witness the harsh conditions the poor had gone through. Because of this inequality issue which has been getting worse, readers are able to put their shoes in the lives of the poor. Such injustice drove to the huge gap between the rich and poor. More importantly, all these unfair treatments have not come to an end. Yet, it was taking advantage of. Since the truth has been revealed in the inner city, New York City, it’s to be spread throughout the world to educate others.<br />Finding my favorite part was difficult because all parts hid some sort or tragic and harsh living conditions. However, regarding the writing structure, there’s no doubt that when reading the conditions of health problems and lack of education privileges, it’s so touching. The imagery was painted so well with the help of setting and plot description. Furthermore, the part where innocent children from desperately poor neighborhoods are dealing with their health issues, it was very touching and memorable. But on the other hand it’s unacceptable how we’re all human but are getting total different treatments and rights.<br />I feel real bad for the new born babies because they had no choice to the type of family they born in. For these children, growing in such bad condition yet, still having to bear witness to the cruel reality was plain harsh! But it was all fate… Anyways, I would highly recommend this to all people as a continuation to A Tree Grows in Brooklyn—a great follow up!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10769589764513699544">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18317925">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15905614354526942585">New York City slums from the perspective of the kids that live there. The author's sincere attempt to describe life for a select group of kids by compiliting the results of numerous interviews. I knew there was extreme poverty in parts of New York City (and crime, drugs, higher rates of AIDS, gangs), but I had no idea that the "public services" (schools, hospitals, parks, and city services) were so, so bad....dirty hospital rooms that patients have to clean themselves, classrooms meeting in bath</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15905614354526942585">New York City slums from the perspective of the kids that live there. The author's sincere attempt to describe life for a select group of kids by compiliting the results of numerous interviews. I knew there was extreme poverty in parts of New York City (and crime, drugs, higher rates of AIDS, gangs), but I had no idea that the "public services" (schools, hospitals, parks, and city services) were so, so bad....dirty hospital rooms that patients have to clean themselves, classrooms meeting in bathrooms, buildings not inspected by the City because inspectors didn't feel 'safe' entering, kids with an average asthma rate 9 times that of kids across town...seriously? You should read this.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15905614354526942585">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer398301079">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9028237712933338284">This book should be read by all. We who are well off can scarcely understand how poor the poor really are. This book brings awareness. The book is difficult to read, given the dire situation described, but is written in such a manner that even humor is thrown in. The book lacks structure; that is why I reduced the stars. <br /><br />And religion? One clearly sees why sometimes people need it. <br /><br />Absolutely perfect narration by Dick Hill.<br /><br />You <u>should</u> read this book. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1320209765">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1320209765?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/19339452-edwin-cruz" class="user" title="Edwin Cruz">Edwin Cruz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1320209765">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer532881744790365878">I was moved by the stories told by the old &amp; young in this community. I truly admire Anthony's (a 13 year old) wisdom and ambition as an aspiring writer. The centrality of the church in providing hope and relief for people in this community reminded me of my own childhood. Despite one's religious beliefs we must never devalue the relationships that people have to spiritual places and the sense of belonging and community that is achieved through these relationships. I respect Kozol for being</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText532881744790365878">I was moved by the stories told by the old &amp; young in this community. I truly admire Anthony's (a 13 year old) wisdom and ambition as an aspiring writer. The centrality of the church in providing hope and relief for people in this community reminded me of my own childhood. Despite one's religious beliefs we must never devalue the relationships that people have to spiritual places and the sense of belonging and community that is achieved through these relationships. I respect Kozol for being able to give voice to each person he befriends and serving more as a messenger. The book ends with an analysis of a sermon with questions to think about: <br /><br />"Will the people Reverend Groover called "the principalities and powers" look into their hearts one day in church or synagogue and feel the grace of God and, as he put it, "be transformed"? Will they become ashamed of what they've done, or what they have accepted? Will they decide they do not need to quarantine the outcasts of their ingenuity and will they then use all their wisdom and their skills to build a new society and new economy in which no human being will be superfluous? I wish I could believe that, but I don't think it is likely. I think it is more likely that they'll write more stories about "Hope Within the Ashes" and then pile on more ashes and then change the subject to the opening of the ballet or a review of a new restaurant. And the children of disappointment will keep dying."<br /><br />I highly recommend this book for anyone who wants a sobering reminder of what many kids go through in low-income communities. It was written in the 90s but its message is still relevant today. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="532881744790365878">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17017982515461223632">I had to read this for a night class where we were to finish it within the span of a week. Being busy with other things, I had two days to start and finish it. I'm literally exhausted from it. This book is powerful, emotional, depressing, macabre and at times even joyful and uplifting. The children that Kozol speaks with are remarkably brilliant and eloquent in their thoughts. Some of them have reached a maturity in their thinking that I don't see in most adults I know. The women and few men tha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17017982515461223632">I had to read this for a night class where we were to finish it within the span of a week. Being busy with other things, I had two days to start and finish it. I'm literally exhausted from it. This book is powerful, emotional, depressing, macabre and at times even joyful and uplifting. The children that Kozol speaks with are remarkably brilliant and eloquent in their thoughts. Some of them have reached a maturity in their thinking that I don't see in most adults I know. The women and few men that he speaks with are equally as amazing, strong and resilient. I have to bite my tongue when I feel the urge to say that Mrs. Washington is, perhaps, my favorite "character" from any book I've read, because I have to remind myself that this book is not fictional. These stories and people are very, very real. Like most, I'm sure, this was my first real glance into the actuality of the destitution that thrives in our country... I think it's an important book that everyone should read. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1215468283">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1215468283" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/22846852-bryon-butler" class="left imgcol" title="Bryon Butler"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1406997464p2/22846852.jpg" alt="Bryon Butler" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1215468283?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 01, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/22846852-bryon-butler" class="user" title="Bryon Butler">Bryon Butler</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1215468283">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2604188042167027940">Years ago, in the mid-1990s, I was in graduate school outside of New York City where the events of Amazing Grace are presented. I made some forays into the Bronx and Yonkers, seeing first hand the plight and injustice so well presented by Jonathan Kozol. As a seminarian, I visited some inner-city churches, most notably the Bronx Household of Faith (bhof.org) and thought of the dedicated, long-suffering pastor who was trying to impact the area. These personal experiences inter-twined with Amazing</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2604188042167027940">Years ago, in the mid-1990s, I was in graduate school outside of New York City where the events of Amazing Grace are presented. I made some forays into the Bronx and Yonkers, seeing first hand the plight and injustice so well presented by Jonathan Kozol. As a seminarian, I visited some inner-city churches, most notably the Bronx Household of Faith (bhof.org) and thought of the dedicated, long-suffering pastor who was trying to impact the area. These personal experiences inter-twined with Amazing Grace, which uncovers the ongoing, non-stopping, never ending, increasingly fraught plight of the largely black and latino masses of the South Bronx. From the schools, to the streets, and into the homeless housing and low-income apartments, violence, poverty, rats, asthma, hunger and a high anxiety induced depression fill the lives of those that public services do not serve. Through individual stories, impacting anecdotes, and an engaging writing style, Kozol convinces the reader of the truth of his accounts.<br />I found it interesting that Kozol, a somewhat secular Jew when he penned the book, highlights the role that Christianity plays in sustaining the lives of some of the characters. It was the same sustaining faith I’d seen in my brief travels there.<br />Before Amazing Grace Jonathan Kozol was one of a myriad of writers that I’d heard of but had never read. I plan to continue with Kozol, whose storytelling and desire for justice are worth the time.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2604188042167027940">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14548816">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11762437105034810701">In 1996 this book changed my life. Because of this book my 16th year became a mission to better the lives of the students of Taft High School (a school that is discussed in the book). I visited the school, the churches and neighborhoods talked about in the book. I established an exchange program between American Fork High and Taft High School that still is in existence today. I highly recommend Jonothon Kozol's Amazing Grace. <br /><br />This book taught me at a young age that I could change the world.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1061184766">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1061184766" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2350772-kate-padilla" class="left imgcol" title="Kate Padilla"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1363662731p2/2350772.jpg" alt="Kate Padilla" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1061184766?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 24, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2350772-kate-padilla" class="user" title="Kate Padilla">Kate Padilla</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1061184766">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11907293403363187233">While the writing was certainly solid and well-executed, I have to say I had a problem with the overall message Kozol was proposing. At the same time that he was accusing white people of segregating themselves from people of other colors (African American and Hispanics, namely), his examples completely segregated those same people of color from white people, simply on the basis of color.<br /><br />In the same way, I found his argument a little one-sided. His book seems to place the situation of the South B</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11907293403363187233">While the writing was certainly solid and well-executed, I have to say I had a problem with the overall message Kozol was proposing. At the same time that he was accusing white people of segregating themselves from people of other colors (African American and Hispanics, namely), his examples completely segregated those same people of color from white people, simply on the basis of color.<br /><br />In the same way, I found his argument a little one-sided. His book seems to place the situation of the South Bronx -- and, by proxy, many ghetto communities across the country -- solely on the shoulders of white, corporate America and the U.S. government. At the same time, he talks about the extreme addiction and violence, the dependence on social welfare programs and the desolation of the local school systems, as if there is no correlation between them. Each is supposed to be individually a result of a corrupted social structure, yet none are supposed to be related to each other. There is no reasoning in his book that would suggest that perhaps (at least a portion) of the responsibility of the current declining structure of the South Bronx may rest on previous generations of the very same people who are suffering. <br /><br />I know it's a little cold to say, but I think it's an important element of the story to consider, and I think he missed looking at his subject with a completely open mind, in order to get a message across.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11907293403363187233">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I was shocked about Teddy Bear Park.....at times a place where drug dealers did their business and government sponsored clean needles were distributed. This was written during the Aids epidemic. It did open my eyes to the placement of incinerators that has caused asthma to be so prevalent for children in the area and hospitals that have overworked doctors and less than sufficient facilities. My views differed from the author in the area that there was no placement of responsibility on the people that lived there. I would be interested to know what has happened since the projects in Chicago has been torn down and the people dispersed. How have they fared? </span>

After this book was released and the treatment became known, not sure if those mentioned in the book were treated better or if life continued as it did before the book.<br /><br />It was difficult to read; but it does help to understand that all are not treated the same and what needs to be changed so all are treated the same.</span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1392071101" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5975164-dylan" class="left imgcol" title="Dylan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1424792265p2/5975164.jpg" alt="Dylan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1392071101?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 06, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5975164-dylan" class="user" title="Dylan">Dylan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/13228280-amazing-grace" title="Paperback 9780770435660" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5975164-dylan?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1392071101">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13775929762641363671">This is a riveting, disturbing, depressing book about the lives of poor people, especially children, in the South Bronx. It has touching stories and many, many sad ones and some so painfully unfair that they veer into the absurd - at one point, an AIDS-stricken cancer survivor is denied social assistance apparently because she is "not sick enough". I don't know what you say to that. I don't know what you say to any of this, or do about it, and neither does Kozol from what I can tell. I kept wait</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13775929762641363671">This is a riveting, disturbing, depressing book about the lives of poor people, especially children, in the South Bronx. It has touching stories and many, many sad ones and some so painfully unfair that they veer into the absurd - at one point, an AIDS-stricken cancer survivor is denied social assistance apparently because she is "not sick enough". I don't know what you say to that. I don't know what you say to any of this, or do about it, and neither does Kozol from what I can tell. I kept waiting for him to propose solutions to the crushing structural problems these stories identify, but he offers none, and maybe has none. So it's just stories. I suppose that's all you can ask of a book, but it doesn't seem like enough.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13775929762641363671">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10564066751513984307"> This book Amazing Grace by Jonathan Kozol is one of the greatest books that has received many awards. It was in the New York Times as a highly recommended book. It takes place in America's very own New York City, where people witnessed poverty and the rough times in life. He shows the side of life that people tend to avoid and it just explains to you all the hardships people go through to live a decent life. He also shows you just how unfair the world is, and how badly people have suffered and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10564066751513984307"> This book Amazing Grace by Jonathan Kozol is one of the greatest books that has received many awards. It was in the New York Times as a highly recommended book. It takes place in America's very own New York City, where people witnessed poverty and the rough times in life. He shows the side of life that people tend to avoid and it just explains to you all the hardships people go through to live a decent life. He also shows you just how unfair the world is, and how badly people have suffered and are still suffering. <br /> Reading this book wasn't exactly a joy, because it pains to know everything going on in life, but it really opens up your mind about everything that goes on and makes you feel like you want to help to make a change in people's lives. Because when i was reading the story i felt like there should be a savior of some sort like someone who would try and attempt to help, but no one ever did. I guess that's the real world for you, but it's not always the happiest live a person could live. Although this book was harsh and saddening, it's still a good read because things like this need to be spread throughout the world. It's a very meaningful book!</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_187383457">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/187383457" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5855283-ronald-wise" class="left imgcol" title="Ronald Wise"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1401344138p2/5855283.jpg" alt="Ronald Wise" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/187383457?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 24, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5855283-ronald-wise" class="user" title="Ronald Wise">Ronald Wise</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer187383457">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3358594486471293848">This is a powerful personal study of life in the South Bronx based on the author's visits and repeated conversations with a few individuals living in America's poorest Congressional District. Most of the conversations are with religious personnel, grandmothers, and children. They all talk in their own ways of the daily impact of unprecedented levels of drug addiction, violence, and terminal illness on their lives, community, and city.<br /><br />Kozol's visits and conversations occurred in the early 1990s w</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3358594486471293848">This is a powerful personal study of life in the South Bronx based on the author's visits and repeated conversations with a few individuals living in America's poorest Congressional District. Most of the conversations are with religious personnel, grandmothers, and children. They all talk in their own ways of the daily impact of unprecedented levels of drug addiction, violence, and terminal illness on their lives, community, and city.<br /><br />Kozol's visits and conversations occurred in the early 1990s when the trends were alarming. People in their immediate vicinity regularly dying from fires, gunfire, and AIDS. Many babies being born with AIDS or drug addictions. A large portion of children were being raised by their grandmothers. One woman noted that the victims of epidemics are usually the oldest and youngest, but that an epidemic that targets young adults is especially destructive to a community. A startling portion of the young adult population in this neighborhood was missing from a combination of causes: Lethal violence, drug addiction, incarceration, and AIDS.<br /><br />These interviews took place at the time the new Mayor Giuliani was simultaneously announcing tax cuts for the wealthy and major cuts in public education and social services, with a background of constant chatter on at least one of the city's major local stations about the "savages" and "animals" "breeding" in the poorer boroughs. Surprising was the lack of anger Kozol heard in his interviews. One girl sadly told him it was like "they want us to all just die", but after thinking about it overnight, she corrected herself and said "they just wish we were never born."<br /><br />Kozol and his acquaintances could not have known when this book was written that some things would soon get better -- urban crime dramatically dropped and progress was made in the prevention and treatment of AIDS in the late 1990s. But one of the more dramatic quotes was from a cleric who said that people experienced "small successes" from time to time, but that those were soon forgotten in a sea of despair.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3358594486471293848">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer23823055">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6766022894892389314">Kozol weaves together the tragic and often inspiring stories of Mott Haven’s families and, along the way, builds a strong case against the all-too-common practice of isolating the poor in urban ghettos. As both the author and the subjects of this work eloquently articulate, segregation is bad for the poor and even more damaging to the nation’s spirit. The accounts in Amazing Grace highlight the desperate conditions in these impoverished areas, the inherent inequalities of segregation, and the re</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6766022894892389314">Kozol weaves together the tragic and often inspiring stories of Mott Haven’s families and, along the way, builds a strong case against the all-too-common practice of isolating the poor in urban ghettos. As both the author and the subjects of this work eloquently articulate, segregation is bad for the poor and even more damaging to the nation’s spirit. The accounts in Amazing Grace highlight the desperate conditions in these impoverished areas, the inherent inequalities of segregation, and the resulting feelings of inferiority, that occur in urban ghettos. Perhaps more importantly, Kozol demonstrates that segregation has made the situations of the racially segregated poor virtually invisible, leading vast segments of America to view de facto segregation as a benign result of uncontrollable factors rather than a destructive force that should be reversed.<br /><br />I think this was my favorite quote from the book:<br />“The trouble with miracles . . . is that they don’t happen for most children . . . There is also a degree of danger that, in emphasizing [them] . . . we may seem to be condemning those who don’t have opportunities like these or, if they do, cannot respond to them.” </span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer573800289">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2706637710199935287"><p>Poverty is an over arching social issue that affect people in many ways. Stress has often been associated with poverty. This relationship is explored through Kozol (1995) and his experiences with children in an impoverished neighborhood. The children he describes have bio-psycho-social risk &amp; resilience factors in regards to stress and poverty. Stress and poverty can be put into an ecological perspective utilizing functionalist theory. Functionalist theory is limited in implementing a planed</p></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2706637710199935287"><p>Poverty is an over arching social issue that affect people in many ways. Stress has often been associated with poverty. This relationship is explored through Kozol (1995) and his experiences with children in an impoverished neighborhood. The children he describes have bio-psycho-social risk &amp; resilience factors in regards to stress and poverty. Stress and poverty can be put into an ecological perspective utilizing functionalist theory. Functionalist theory is limited in implementing a planed change process with a client. Utilizing empowerment theory works to both help the client understand there issue and they aren't the only one with the issue (much like functionalism). It goes further to help create a planned change process for the client and to continue that process at the macro level.</p><br /><br /><p>You can read the entire article in my resources section of my website, <a rel="nofollow" href="http://jacobrcampbell.com/resources/articles/stress-poverty-case-study-using-amazing-grace">The Stress of Poverty: A Case Study Using Amazing Grace</a> or you can read <a rel="nofollow" href="http://jacobrcampbell.com/tags/book-reviews">more reviews</a>.</p></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_182149745">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182149745" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4127317-katie" class="left imgcol" title="Katie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1297086744p2/4127317.jpg" alt="Katie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/182149745?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 12, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4127317-katie" class="user" title="Katie">Katie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer182149745">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9437291865979926267">Although Jonathan Kozol wrote and researched this book almost 20 years ago (when I was just a little girl), sadly, so much of it still rings true today. The primary focus of this book pertains to the lives of children within the South Bronx. Kozol particularly emphasizes the ways in which these young children process and understand the world in which they live, as well as their places within it. Whenever I'm reading, especially nonfiction texts, I often highlight quotations that I feel are signi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9437291865979926267">Although Jonathan Kozol wrote and researched this book almost 20 years ago (when I was just a little girl), sadly, so much of it still rings true today. The primary focus of this book pertains to the lives of children within the South Bronx. Kozol particularly emphasizes the ways in which these young children process and understand the world in which they live, as well as their places within it. Whenever I'm reading, especially nonfiction texts, I often highlight quotations that I feel are significant, startling, etc. Below I have included some of these quotations; my hope is that they will convince you to read this book if you have not yet done so.<br /><br />The following are things that various residents, along with religious leaders and teachers, of the South Bronx, said to Kozol during interviews. Most often, the "they" referred to in these quotations are the leaders and citizens of New York City.<br /><br />--"[They] don't clean up your neighborhood, don't fix your buildings, fix your schools, or give you decent hospitals or banks. Instead, [they] paint the back sides of the buildings so that people driving to the suburbs will have something nice to look at."<br />--"It's not like being in a jail. It's more like being 'hidden.' It's as if you have been put in a garage where, if they don't have room for something but aren't sure if they should throw it out, they put it where they don't need to think of it again."<br />--"If you weave enough bad things into the fibers of a person's life--sickness and filth, old mattresses and other junk thrown in the streets and other ugly ruined things, and ruined people, a prison here, sewage there, drug dealers here, the homeless people over there, then give us the very worst schools anyone could think of, hospitals that keep you waiting for ten hours, police that don't show up when someone's dying, take the train that's underneath the street in the good neighborhoods and put it up above where it shuts out the sun, you can guess that life will not be very nice and children will not have much sense of being glad of who they are. Sometimes it feels like we've been buried six feel under their perceptions."<br />--"You see destruction around you, but you do not know who the destroyer is."<br />--"Cops think of the building like a death camp. But if the police are scared to come there, why does the city put small children in the building?"<br />--"There is nothing predatory in these children. They know that the world does not much like them and they try hard to be good to one another."<br />--"You have to remember that for this little boy whom you have met, his life is just as important, to him, as your life is to you. No matter how insufficient or how shabby it may seem to some, it is the only one he has."<br />--"Sometimes, in front of a wonderful place like FAO Schwartz, you wonder if poor kids like these have fantasies of breaking in and stealing toys or games, electric trains--whatever children play with nowadays. If they ever did it, if they just went in one night and cleaned the whole place out, you have to ask if they could ever steal back half as much as has been stolen from them."<br />--"I think it grieves the heart of God when human beings created in His image treat other human beings like filthy rags."<br />--"Another boy, I used to ask him, 'Where do you want to go eat?' He always said, 'McDonald's.' One time, when he was 12, I took him to a Burger King in Queens. I later learned from his teacher that he wrote an essay on our lunch, 'My Trip to Burger King'--the way that wealthy kids might write about a trip to Florida."<br />--"This out here is not God's kingdom. A kingdom is a place of glory. This is a place of pain."<br />--"Every child among us has a precious life and holds a precious dream."<br />--"Going outside for youngsters in the building means going to the hallway, since the road outside, where they could get some air, is just too dangerous."<br />--"I like to look at children on the train. You don't see many people who look friendly on the train. But children do. Some of them do. Some of them look joyful. Some of them say hello to you, even to strangers. No one else does. They want to be loved."<br />--"With his intelligence, he could have been successful in an honest occupation. But he had nothing in his life but drugs."<br />--"'Traumatization' as an ordinary state of mind is closer to the fact of things for many children here. They lead the life most people only read about."<br />--"Many of the ambitions of the children are locked-in at a level that suburban kids would scorn. It's as if the very possibilities of life have been scaled back. Boys who are doing well in school will tell me, 'I would like to be a sanitation man.' I have to guard my words and not say anything to indicate my disappointment. In this neighborhood, a sanitation job is something to be longed for."<br />--"If you want to get your outcasts out of sight, first you need a ghetto and then you need a prison to take pressure off the ghetto. The fact that it doesn't make financial sense is not the point. Short-term terror and revulsion are more powerful than long-term wisdom or self-interest."<br />--"Bigotry is not the only factor in the flight of some white children from these schools. Many of their parents simply don't believe these schools are good for any child, their own or anybody else's. So they put their own kids into private schools and try to raise some scholarships to pay for black kids to attend them, too. But it tends to be a triage operation. The black kids who get into private schools like these are screened quite carefully. So, in one sense, it simply makes things worse in public schools by pulling out the children that a teacher counts on to keep class discussions going and to spur the others to succeed."<br />--"The view of the United States that children get in looking out the window of a school in Harlem or the Bronx is not one likely to affirm a sense of confidence in human goodness."<br />--"Down south people let you know exactly where you stand. Here in New York they smile and smile and pat you on the head and then they send you back where you belong."<br />--"If a woman's black, Hispanic, and on welfare, maybe a drug user, or has HIV, she knows she isn't welcome in a first-class hospital...If they wouldn't want you as a neighbor, why do you think they'd want you in the next bed?"<br />--"Keepin' a man is not the biggest problem. Keepin' from bein' killed is bigger. Keepin' your kids alive is bigger. If nothin' else works, why should a marriage work?"<br />--"Of course the family structure breaks down in a place like the South Bronx! Everything breaks down in a place like this. The pipes break down. The phone breaks down. The electricity and heat break down. The spirit breaks down. The body breaks down. The immune agents of the hearts break down. Why wouldn't the family break down also? If we saw the people in these neighborhoods as part of the same human family to which we belong, we'd never put them in such places to begin with. But we do not think of them that way. That is one area of 'family breakdown' that the experts and newspapers seldom speak of. They speak of the failures of the mothers we have exiled to do well within their place of exile. They do not condemn the pharaoh."<br />--"It has to take extraordinary self-deceit for people who plant flowers on Park Avenue but pump their sewage into Harlem and transport their medical waste, and every other kind of waste that you can think of, to Mott Haven, to image that they have the moral standing to be judges of the people they have segregated and concealed. Only a very glazed and clever culture in which social blindness is accepted as a normal state of mind could possibly permit itself this luxury."<br />--"The viral path of AIDS has crept through the family tree in many South Bronx neighborhoods, breaking branch after branch."<br />--"Do I need this bottle of expensive perfume more than a child needs a doctor or a decent school? What does it mean, in theological terms, when grown-ups can eat caviar while Anthony eats oatmeal? What does this say about a city's soul?"<br /><br /><br />The following are a variety of observations that Kozol makes throughout the book:<br />--"Jeremiah and his friends do not speak during our meeting with the jargon that some middle-class Americans identify with inner-city kids. There's no obscenity in their speech, nor are there any of those flip code-phrases that are almost always placed within the mouths of poor black children in the movies--a style of speech, I sometimes think, that may be exaggerated by the media to lend a heightened sense of 'differentness' to children in the ghetto."<br />--"In a deep side gutter, one small child, wearing only underpants, lies on his stomach in a pool of dirty water about six inches deep, slashing joyfully, pretending he can swim."<br />--"This is where Bernardo played for eight and a half years. This is the best New York could do for him. The kennel where I leave my dog while I am in New York is cleaner and smells better."<br />--"Anabelle's images of heaven give me a delightful feeling that I rarely have in New York City. I speak of these kinds of things as often as I can, and of the feelings children voice for animals they love, because I think they show us something very different from the customary picture we are given of a generation of young thugs and future whores. There is a golden moment here that our society has chosen not to seize. We have not nourished this part of the hearts of children, not in New York, not really anywhere."<br />--"In order to keep these different children clear in my mind, I finally have to make a map of the South Bronx and put it on the wall over my desk, placing a marker on each block in which a child died, using one symbol for death by fire, one for death by accident, and one for death by gunshot."<br />--"Twice as many black men in New York are under control of the criminal justice system as are enrolled full-time in all the colleges within the state."<br />--"It is at the secondary level--in junior high and, more dramatically, in high school--that the sense of human ruin on a vast scale becomes unmistakable. Numbers cannot convey the mood of desolation that pervades some of these secondary schools."<br />--"At one junior high school in the South Bronx in which money was so scarce in 1994 that girls were using pieces of TV cable as their jump ropes at the time I visited the area, only 15 teachers in a faculty of 54 were certified."<br />--"So long as the most vulnerable people in our population are consigned to places that the rest of us will always shun and flee and view with fear, I am afraid that educational denial, medical and economic devastation, and aesthetic degradation will be virtually inevitable...So long as there are ghetto neighborhoods and ghetto hospitals and ghetto schools, I am convinced there will be ghetto desperation, ghetto violence, and ghetto fear because a ghetto is itself an evil and unnatural construction."<br />--"One wants instead to know how certain people hold up under terrible ordeals, how many more do not, how human beings devalue other people's lives, how numbness and destructiveness are universalized, how human pity is at length extinguished and the shunning of the vulnerable can come in time to be perceived as natural behavior...How does a nation deal with those whom it has cursed?"<br />--"A handful of good, publicly funded clinics, which are perennially overcrowded, try to compensate for the abandonment of New York City's poorest children by much of its medical establishment."<br />--"239 of 277 swings for children in Bronx parks aren't 'in place' or 'need repair.' Trivial as it is, this disappointing detail seems to say it all."</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9437291865979926267">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_79514435">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/79514435?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 03, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/671457-carin" class="user" title="Carin">Carin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/671457-carin?shelf=education" class="actionLinkLite">education</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/671457-carin?shelf=political" class="actionLinkLite">political</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/671457-carin?shelf=sociology" class="actionLinkLite">sociology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer79514435">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18037023385656611617">This book wasn't exactly what I was expecting because with Mr. Kozol, I figured it would mostly be about education. But that's about the only thing he doesn't really touch on much in this book. Mostly it's about a single neighborhood in the Bronx: Mott Haven. I found it telling that I lived in New York City for almost five years, in Queens, and I never even heard of this neighborhood.<br /><br />Given the amount of crime in this neighborhood, you'd think it would be on the news every night, but given the co</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18037023385656611617">This book wasn't exactly what I was expecting because with Mr. Kozol, I figured it would mostly be about education. But that's about the only thing he doesn't really touch on much in this book. Mostly it's about a single neighborhood in the Bronx: Mott Haven. I found it telling that I lived in New York City for almost five years, in Queens, and I never even heard of this neighborhood.<br /><br />Given the amount of crime in this neighborhood, you'd think it would be on the news every night, but given the commonality of that crime, it wasn't. In this book, Mr. Kozol gets to know a few dozen members of this community, focusing on the children, and tells us what their lives are like, how they get by, what their options are (or aren't), and a little bit about how their lives got this way. Along the way, many people, including many children, die. Yet there are stories of hope, even if they are few and far between. I was particularly touched by the drug kingpin who declared no one was to be selling any drugs during the hour when children are walking home from school. And also in the local "park"--although it seemed more like an empty lot--there was a tree with teddy bears hanging from branches that was an unofficial safe place where mothers could play with their children and the dealers and gang members would leave them alone.<br /><br />Overall this was a fascinating but depressing book. The only hope was that some people could get out of there. There wasn't any hope for the neighborhood generally. But what else do we expect in an area with no/terrible access to medical facilities, awful poorly funded schools, no jobs, falling-down buildings police won't even go in. This book was published in 1995, so I do hope there have been some improvements, but I am not holding my breath. I truly admire Mr. Kozol for giving voice to a segment of society usually ignored by the rest of us. But we can't fix the problems we don't see.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18037023385656611617">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5449847411570937889">I think this book would have had much more of an impact if I'd read it back in 1995 when it was first published. The cold sad truth is that these stories are now all-too-familiar in many impoverished neighborhoods around our nation including in my own city of Birmingham, Alabama. Although I have not studied the statistics, my gut tells me that the income gap between the poorest poor and the richest rich in New York City has widened substantially since this book was written. The New York Times, h</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5449847411570937889">I think this book would have had much more of an impact if I'd read it back in 1995 when it was first published. The cold sad truth is that these stories are now all-too-familiar in many impoverished neighborhoods around our nation including in my own city of Birmingham, Alabama. Although I have not studied the statistics, my gut tells me that the income gap between the poorest poor and the richest rich in New York City has widened substantially since this book was written. The New York Times, however, reported just this year about the number of middle class professionals, many of them white, moving into the neighborhoods described in Jonathan Kozol's book. Apparently the attraction is affordable real estate, an increasingly safe neighborhood where major crime has plummeted over the past 20 years, and a reasonable commute to jobs in Manhatten. I'm pleased to hear that the reputation of the neighborhood is changing for what appears to be the better but I would be very interested in a follow up book on the children highlighted in "Amazing Grace." Where are they today and have those precious children benefited from the enhancements and improvements in their neighborhood? My prayers may have already been answered as Kozol's newest book, Fire in the Ashes, which was published in August 2012 does just that. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_63201745">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63201745" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/521334-hong-deng" class="left imgcol" title="Hong Deng"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1206928937p2/521334.jpg" alt="Hong Deng" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/63201745?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 20, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/521334-hong-deng" class="user" title="Hong Deng">Hong Deng</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/521334-hong-deng?shelf=11th-grade-advisory-school-year" class="actionLinkLite">11th-grade-advisory-school-year</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer63201745">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17970760122356698912">Through the words of many children, and parents, <u>Amazing Grace </u>reveals the painful lives of those who settled in South Bronx in the late 1990s. The neighborhoods in South Bronx were soared with impoverished families who constantly faced brutal violence and sickness. The stories of these people resonate with the unjust treatments and inequalities the poor received throughout the world. Giving countless touching examples of intelligent children and hardworking adults who resigned to the atrocities</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17970760122356698912">Through the words of many children, and parents, <u>Amazing Grace </u>reveals the painful lives of those who settled in South Bronx in the late 1990s. The neighborhoods in South Bronx were soared with impoverished families who constantly faced brutal violence and sickness. The stories of these people resonate with the unjust treatments and inequalities the poor received throughout the world. Giving countless touching examples of intelligent children and hardworking adults who resigned to the atrocities established and forced upon them by the society, Kozol disproved the argument presented by many social scientists that “the largest portion of the suffering poor people undergo has to be blamed upon their own behaviors”. Indeed, many people might suffer from poverty due to their own behaviors such as not being able to get a good education because of one’s unwillingness to work hard. But many also undergo poverty because of forces in the society. A kid who is born with AIDs obviously cannot achieve as much as a healthy kid who is prepared to cope with physical and mental challenges in learning. <br /><br />“I wonder how powerful God is,” says David, one of the children from South Bronx that Kozol interviewed. “He must be wise and powerful to make the animals and trees and give man organs and a brain to build complex machineries, but He is not powerful enough to stop the evil on the earth, to change the hearts of people.” What David said matches perfectly well to what I felt when I finished the book. I couldn’t help but also doubt in the power of God. If God created everyone equally then why are some people born with wealth and prestige while others are bound to be suffered? During Kozol’s interview with a group of kids from a church in South Bronx, one of the girls described how the government tried to fool tourists of the New York City by giving a beautiful paint on the outside of their houses. The kids expressed their thought that they were being isolated and hidden in the unhappy neighborhoods in the U.S. I’m happy that in recent years, the government has put more effort to provide a substantial education for kids of minorities and of the poor. <br /><br />This book was certainly impressive yet painful to finish. It raised many ethical questions that made me pondered for a long time. It also made me realized how fortunate I am to have a great education, a happy family, and to be able to enrich my life with different entertainments. But I also wish I can be like Kozol, who has the ability to reach out to those who are less fortunate. I believe no matter what are fates might be, each individual has the responsibility to help out others. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17970760122356698912">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_140454798">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/140454798" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/454236-heidi" class="left imgcol" title="Heidi"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1422623715p2/454236.jpg" alt="Heidi" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/140454798?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 13, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/454236-heidi" class="user" title="Heidi">Heidi</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/454236-heidi?shelf=nonfiction" class="actionLinkLite">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/454236-heidi?shelf=social-issues" class="actionLinkLite">social-issues</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer140454798">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1993262897891195964">This was, hands down, one of the most depressing books I've ever read. Author Jonathan Kozol visits neighborhoods in the South Bronx, one of the poorest areas of the United States, and talks with many of the people who live there about their lives, and it is astonishing. Poverty, drugs, prostitution, gun violence, AIDS, rat infestations -- all make their home among crumbling infrastructure in neighborhoods where public safety and education are neglected by those in charge, sharing space with chi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1993262897891195964">This was, hands down, one of the most depressing books I've ever read. Author Jonathan Kozol visits neighborhoods in the South Bronx, one of the poorest areas of the United States, and talks with many of the people who live there about their lives, and it is astonishing. Poverty, drugs, prostitution, gun violence, AIDS, rat infestations -- all make their home among crumbling infrastructure in neighborhoods where public safety and education are neglected by those in charge, sharing space with children who know no other kind of life, and perhaps never will.<br /><br />Reagan popularized the image of the Welfare Queen in the '80s; in the current economic climate, prominent politicians and political candidates have suggested that the unemployed are just lazy; heated debate is still going on in the US as to whether or not access to health care is a right or a privilege. After reading "Amazing Grace" I'm more convinced than ever that poverty, true poverty, is seriously misunderstood in this country. According to this book New York City has basically established a self-perpetuating system of poverty in some areas, and has proceeded to pretend that such places do not exist and do not need substantial assistance. It's preposterous that people in one of the richest cities in one of the richest countries in the world should live and die in such filthy, dangerous conditions because the city wants to save money.<br /><br />Most of the book is comprised only of Kozol's observations of the neighborhood, and conversations with residents there, with very little editorializing or speculation on the author's part. When I first finished the book I was unhappy with the way Kozol chose to end it: there's no silver lining to the situation of the people he describes, there's no easy fix to these problems. But upon reflection I appreciate the fact that he didn't try to sugarcoat things. It was terribly sad, but felt completely honest. <br /><br />This book is over 15 years old now, so I've spent a lot of time wondering if and how things have changed in the intervening years for the people Kozol met. If the recent economic disasters in the US are anything to go by, I have to assume things have not magically improved in the South Bronx. I won't try to pretend I know anything about how best to deal with this kind of poverty, but this book was really eye-opening in exposing me to its very existence within the US. Maybe if more people were aware, from the work of people like Kozol, maybe then something would be able to change.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1993262897891195964">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/5629511?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 04, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/316163-hm" class="user" title="HM">HM</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer5629511">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17903202190401574609">I had to read this book the summer before going to freshman year of college at HOly Cross and I fell in love with it. <br /><br />Again, Kozol is one of my writing heroes...and his style is easy to read and full of details that really help you understand his point of view. <br /><br />Here's what the publisher said about this book: <br /><br />The children in this book defy the stereotypes of urban youth too frequently presented by the media. Tender, generous and often religiously devout, they speak with eloquence and honesty abou</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17903202190401574609">I had to read this book the summer before going to freshman year of college at HOly Cross and I fell in love with it. <br /><br />Again, Kozol is one of my writing heroes...and his style is easy to read and full of details that really help you understand his point of view. <br /><br />Here's what the publisher said about this book: <br /><br />The children in this book defy the stereotypes of urban youth too frequently presented by the media. Tender, generous and often religiously devout, they speak with eloquence and honesty about the poverty and racial isolation that have wounded but not hardened them.<br /><br />The book does not romanticize or soften the effects of violence and sickness. One fourth of the child-bearing women in the neighborhoods where these children live test positive for HIV. Pediatric AIDs, life-consuming fires and gang rivalries take a high toll. Several children die during the year in which this narrative takes place.<br /><br />A gently written work, Amazing Grace asks questions that are at once political and theological. What is the value of a child's life? What exactly do we plan to do with those whom we appear to have defined as economically and humanly superfluous? How cold -- how cruel, how tough -- do we dare be? </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17903202190401574609">...more</a>

  <a href="/user/show/417085-tia" class="left imgcol" title="Tia"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1398371098p2/417085.jpg" alt="Tia" /></a>

      <a href="/user/show/417085-tia" class="user" title="Tia">Tia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tia by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2926674-katie">Katie</a>
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/417085-tia?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/417085-tia?shelf=nonfiction" class="actionLinkLite">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/417085-tia?shelf=african-american" class="actionLinkLite">african-american</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer251697201">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14177334424425404137">Despite having just left the "front lines" of urban education and finding little in this book surprising, <em>Amazing Grace</em> is a powerful read. <br /><br />The book begins with transcribed conversations from urban youth, parents, and community leaders in one of America's worst neighborhoods in the nineties. Slowly, Kozol begins to translate and offer insight into their words with statistics and comparisons he finds from his research. This insight all points to the racial segregation of New York City, which resi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14177334424425404137">Despite having just left the "front lines" of urban education and finding little in this book surprising, <em>Amazing Grace</em> is a powerful read. <br /><br />The book begins with transcribed conversations from urban youth, parents, and community leaders in one of America's worst neighborhoods in the nineties. Slowly, Kozol begins to translate and offer insight into their words with statistics and comparisons he finds from his research. This insight all points to the racial segregation of New York City, which residents acknowledge but refuse to address.<br /><br />In the final chapter, he discusses with a reverand a letter distributed by the transit system that asks residents not to give money to beggars - to instead look in the yellow pages for "Human Services." When he asks the reverand if she thinks people will actually look in the yellow pages, she says no:<br /><br />"I don't think that that's the purpose of it anyway. I don't think the point is charity but self-protection. I mean, emotional protection. Looking into the eyes of a poor person is upsetting because normal people have a conscience. Touching the begger's hand, meeting his gaze, makes a connection. It locks you in. It makes it hard to sleep ..."<br /><br />This book locks you in, as well. The amount of problems and potential sources of those problems seem endless. As you read further, you can feel that weight bearing down on Kozol, as his conversations and thoughts stray further from his original intent and eventually abandon an argument altogether. The book seems to say, <em>What argument is there to make?</em>, so, instead, Kozol ends with the blunt honesty he's come to adore from the urban families he's befriended:<br /><br />"I don't know what can change this."</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14177334424425404137">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1035798035">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1035798035" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/32458103-luke-o-leary" class="left imgcol" title="Luke O'leary"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1403394951p2/32458103.jpg" alt="Luke O'leary" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1035798035?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 24, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/32458103-luke-o-leary" class="user" title="Luke O'leary">Luke O'leary</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1035798035">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7568887929759672328">Jonathan Kozol gives a genuine effort to expose the reality of the New York slums. A reality that the average American either chooses to ignore, or - in my case - never knew about. Surely, everyone knows that poverty exists in America. But, this book takes the guts of all the truth of poverty in the U.S. and throws it into your face. As dramatic as that may sound, that's really how this book goes. That said, it is completely eye-opening and gives a greater perspective to anyone who reads it.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lU90Y9q3Kaq+acZ40o4WTNuDnqChJeIQjeyRH2luDlbwm0uwKGU9UDKxssOs7/D+knRduej4pbrn5ZSNpfv4Ew==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hqN8mjYnbcQCwqoh54bROVKwSMq27Z/DVTmH64+SVJ7jd0NJxPV5Po4a3pqZ5zeLG0eL0/8w2Gk/MIJ5Qwei2w==')})
  }
//]]&gt;
</script>
